Abstract
This work is motivated by the possible need to factor in changes in a boltвАЩs boundary when
estimating its tension, as the end-points of such a bolt will be highly dependent on tension. A
purely academic case of a generic linear homogeneous beam in tension with linear springs and
dampers at its boundary will be studied. The purpose is to use vibrations data to identify
or estimate linear or non-linear stiпђАness and damping eпђАects at boundaries. Estimation of
linear boundary conditions will be studied, where estimation is done by пђБtting boundary
parameters of a model to measured vibrations data in various ways. Non-linear boundary
identiпђБcation will be studied as well, using time-series vibrations data. Methods suggested
in this report will be validated on simulated or experimental data. The suggested methods
works well when boundary parameters are not an extreme case (representing either inпђБnite
stiпђАness, inпђБnite compliance, zero damping ect.). Several scripts have been created that can
пђБt beam boundary parameters to measured data and identify non-linearity in a time signals
governing dynamics. These can be used on measured data from vibrations of general beam
structures to gather information about boundaries.
Preface
This project was introduced as an idea by Jon Juel Thomsen in context with DTUвАЩs VEBJI
project, a project concerned with estimating bolted joint integrity. We had met together
with my other supervisor, Si Mohammed Sah, to discuss the topic of my master thesis, which
is a пђБve month project.
The projects purpose was to study how to incorporate a bolted joints changes in properties
as it is loosened, by studying a purely academic case of a generic beam with arbitrary
boundaries.
The project started with a research into current techniques on the subject, and I was
inspired to develop identiпђБcation procedures that could identify any non-linear boundary
condition. From the start I set high ambitions, believing that I would master linear estimation
techniques in a month or two, and then spend most of my time on non-linear boundary
identiпђБcation. But I was wrong, even spending the пђБrst month on dead ends. Almost all my
time has been spent on linear boundary estimations, because I was convinced by supervisors
to start with doing something simple.
However, my ambition to research non-linearities, which were an actual interest of mine,
drove me to want to пђБnish linear boundary estimation quicker. In this regard, my project
was governed by FickвАЩs law. The rate of change of gathered information was proportional to
the diпђАerences between where I am and where I want to be. Every week I would try to make
sure I was on schedule with the project plan I had made, so that I could show something
to my supervisors on our weekly meetings. This stressed me somewhat, even into believing
that I was behind. What annoyed me the most, was when my supervisors demanded that
I validated the various hypothesisвАЩ I had made. This slowed me down, but it also led me
to discover many interesting observations and un-robustness to uncertainty. Often it would
disprove my hypothesisвАЩ, which was incredibly annoying. Several dead ends are not included
in this report because they were disproved. However, sometimes the opposite was the case,
==PAGE:4==
iv
my original tests invalidated hypothesisвАЩ, but later was found out to be just in wrong ranges
for the hypothesis to be valid. In these cases, the scientiпђБc method automatically helped pull
the report in a direction so that I am certain that whatвАЩs in this report is very certain. In
any case, FickвАЩs law for project work should be supplemented with a damping term that is
proportional to the velocity of the project.
My research process was somewhat independent. This is mostly because I enjoy trying
to make derivations myself. I had to force myself to look up solutions to existing problems,
which often would give me an easy answer - which would be boring. However, it also meant
that I did not repeat anything that was easily found by literature searches, also it broadened
my horizon by forcing me to read a lot of papers and learn about inspiring ingenious analysis
tools.
The report is structured as constantly building up to something slightly more complicated,
starting with simple analysis of a problem with many knowns. Sections 2.1 and 2.2 must be
read initially, as they formulate the case study of this report. But sections 2.3, 2.4, 2.5-2.6,
2.7-3.2 and Chapter 4 have been designed to be read somewhat independently of each other.
Sections 2.6-4.3 form the primary contributions of this report, while the пђБrst sections 2.3-2.5
illustrate some qualitative results that is obscured by complexity in later sections, but still
remain true.

Chapter 1
Introduction
This chapter describes the motivation of this work, a literature review on already existing
research on the subject and is rounded oпђАby a project plan.
1.1
Problem Formulation
The purpose of this project is to identify models and estimate stiпђАness and damping pa-
rameters, describing boundary conditions of elastic beams using simulated and measured
vibrations.
1.2
Motivation
This work is a contribution to the larger research project вАЭVibrations for Estimating Bolted
Joint IntegrityвАЭ conducted by Denmarks Technical University, Karlsr¬®uhe Institute of Tech-
nology and Br¬®uel and Kj√¶r Sound and Vibration.
The motivation of this work is the desire to understand the dynamics of bolted joints,
which sometimes appear as boundary conditions for beams. These joints are complicated
to model, but understanding their dynamics would enable techniques for detection of bolt
integrity. Bolt integrity measurements are today carried out with amongst others, torque
wrenches, sensor washers, loosening angle measurements and ultrasonic measurement tech-
niques [4]. These existing bolt integrity measurement techniques may be impractical and/or
resource-consuming.
1.3
Background
This work builds on solid mechanics: beam theory, modal analysis, nonlinear vibrations and
computational vibrations [1-2].Treatments of bolt stress distributions can be found in [3],
which is relevant for understanding how a bolt grips. Bolt-tightening literature exists in [4]
detailing tightening techniques such as hydraulic tightening, and tightening using a heating
rod.
1
==PAGE:14==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Work have been dedicated to bolt tension estimation at DTU Mechanical Engineering,
through transient analysis of natural frequencies [8], damping [9], psychoacoustics [9], spectral
centroid [9] and decay rates [9]. On general beams, axial force estimation has been carried
out by using natural frequencies [10] and using mode shapes [11]. Alternative measures of
bolt tension, using ultrasonic wave speeds as indicator of bolt pre-stresses exists [12].
Bolt experiments have been described by [13], which showed through empirical mode
decomposition, that in their setup, bolted joint damping decayed linearly with time. Work
has been dedicated to FE modeling of bolt friction, which has several numerical issues due
to non-linearity [14]. Frequency response analysis of mechanical systems with a micro-slip
friction model has been conducted [15], with its eпђАect on stiпђАness and damping accounted
for. The literature presents treatments of static friction modeling of the relation between
bolt tightening torque and bolt tension [3].
Modeling of friction using the stick-slip method is treated in the literature [16-20] - this
method models dry friction as either вАЭslippingвАЭ with a constant slipping force, or вАЭstickingвАЭ
with a non-constant sticking force which holds the otherwise moving object in place. Another
model, macro and micro-slip exists, and treatments can be found including application to
bolted joints [21-23]. Micro- and macro-slip models are used to treat cases with non-uniform
pressure on contact surfaces. When pressure is non-uniform, some areas of the contact may
be in stick-mode, and some may be in slip-mode.
When non-linear boundary conditions are examined, it is relevant to also research litera-
ture on other nonlinear phenomenon in beams. Beam non-linearities include static non-linear
boundary conditions [24], torsional vibration coupling [25] and axial-transverse vibration cou-
pling among others. Axial and transverse vibration coupling is relevant for detecting loose
bolts - as vibration in one direction may loosen the bolt, aпђАecting vibrations in other direc-
tions.
Beams have many degrees of freedom and can be modeled using modal analysis [1,2].
Frequency response measurements can be used to identify linear parameters and identify
non-linearities and пђБt non-linear models to frequency responses [26]. Harmonically excited
non-linear modes can be modeled [27]. Axial forces in beams induce non-linearity (stress-
stiпђАening) - these non-linearities has been treated using analytical and numerical methods
[28].
An empirical model of bolt stiпђАness to be used as a boundary condition is has found in
the literature [29] - the model is a continuous approximation of a stiпђАness that is zero for no
bolt grip and inпђБnite for high bolt grips. Axial forces can be estimated without knowledge
of boundary conditions using 5 mode shapes measurements of a single mode [30].
A diпђГcult aspect of bolt-friction is hysteresis. The topic of hysteresis has general treat-
ments in the literature [47,54]. Treatments of an almost general model for hysteresis, the
Bouc-Wen model can be found in several articles [45,46]. The literature also treats loss factor
hysteresis [48]. Loss factor hysteresis can also be modeled by using the Hilbert transform
[49,55]. Many hysteresis models (Bouc-Wen, etc) use internal degrees of freedom in its nu-
merics - these are not measurable. The complexity of such models can be avoided by using
loss factor hysteresis, but accuracy and physical interpretations may be lost.
Standard textbooks treat general system identiпђБcation theory for dynamical systems [5],
including treatments of identiпђБcation of non-linearities [6] is referred to. Many identiпђБcation
procedures exists, including but not limited to restoring force surface [52,53,56,7], empirical
==PAGE:15==
1.4. THIS PROJECT
3
mode decomposition, structural model updating, detection of harmonics arising from non-
linearity and many other methods. All the mentioned methods can be used as tools in this
work.
The пђБeld of control theory has its own estimation procedures, which are required to be
real-time during measurements. The methods are based on minimal noise principles [31].
When a system is governed by non-linear relations, and the structure of these relations are
unknown, a fusion of control methods and the restoring force surface method can be used to
identify and estimate the parameters of the unknown structure [32].
Non-linear estimation without a priori knowledge of equation structure can also be found
in the literature of neural networks. Several researchers have already used neural networks
to monitor defects [33-35]. MATLAB supports the use of neural networks with a toolbox
[36]. Hysteresis observers are treated in the literature [50-51]. However, since control theory
methods are on-line estimation, they may not be as eпђГcient as oпђА-line estimators, such as
restoring force surface methods.
Multiple parameter estimation can be applied on simple mechanical systems by using the
method of escort probabilities[37].
Theory of the empirical mode decomposition method by Huang et. al [38] are applied in
several papers [39-40], including applying the EMD-method and Hilbert Spectrum analysis
on beam vibrations with a cubic boundary condition. Empirical mode decomposition is a
time series analysis tool, which allows for analysis of non-stationary data.
This method
is relevant for treating non-linearity, which has non-stationary natural frequencies (due to
amplitude-dependence) and/or damping. Hilbert Spectrum Analysis is an analysis that can
recover both linear natural frequencies and amplitudes of a vibration signal, but can also
identify amplitude and frequency modulations, meaning it can be used non-linear analysis
(because non-linearities cause amplitudes and natural frequencies to be non-constant [1])
Literature exists on system identiпђБcation using spectral analysis on random signals of
MISO-systems [41-44]. These methods are useful for analyzing data from experiments with
random excitation. The methods also have the advantage of using correlations to decouple
linear and non-linear eпђАects of an input to an output.
Finally, general parameter estimation theory (linear and non-linear) can be found in
textbooks [57], which is also be relevant for identifying boundaries. Estimation of beam
parameters (element stiпђАness and inertia [58], clamped boundary stiпђАness [59]) using least
squares on experimental data is found in the literature.
1.4
This project
One of the aims of this project is beam boundary stiпђАness and damping parameter estimation.
General estimation procedures exists for generic problems [57], and parameter estimation
techniques have been carried out on beams [58-59], but not for boundary parameters of
general boundaries. Original parameter estimation techniques for boundary parameters is
needed. Another aim of this project is identifying non-linear beam boundary eпђАects. While
some work has been dedicated to this topic for a beam with one non-linear (cubic) boundary
[40], it is needed to apply these existing techniques on a more general case.
For this project, a technique for estimating linear boundary parameters and tension of
==PAGE:16==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
beams will be tested and developed, based on existing parameter estimation techniques [57].
The estimation techniques will use experimentally found natural frequencies, damping ratios
and mode shapes to estimate boundary parameters and tension. Empirical mode decomposi-
tion will be applied to time-signals from beam vibrations in order to isolate intrinsic mode
functions from a signal that has inпђБnitely many modes. Then, by considering the amplitude
and frequency variations of such a mode, non-linear eпђАects of boundaries will be identiпђБed.
Isolating intrinsic mode functions and identifying non-linear boundaries will be based on the
work of Kurt et. al [40].
1.5
Project Plan
First step for this project will be to develop and test estimation procedures for linear param-
eters of boundaries. To be able to estimate boundary stiпђАness and tension parameters from
natural frequencies, пђБrst boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ eпђАect on the natural frequencies of beams will be
analyzed using analytical methods. With the insight of this analysis, estimation techniques
based on least square methods that пђБt boundary stiпђАness and damping parameters and ten-
sion to experimental natural frequencies, mode shapes and damping ratios will be developed
and tested to estimate.
Once linear parameter estimation techniques have been developed, the eпђАects of bound-
ary non-linearities on vibrations will be analyzed. Given the insight from this analysis, the
identiпђБcation techniques proposed by Kurt et. al [40] will be tuned to this problem. These
identiпђБcation techniques consists of processing time series using empirical mode decomposi-
tion, then using Hilbert Spectrum analysis to quantify non-linearity. The methods will be
tested for simpliпђБed beam models (one and two degrees of freedom beams).
The plan can be summarized as:
1 Analyze eпђАects of linear boundary conditions of a beam on natural frequencies and
damping ratios
2 Estimate linear boundary parameters and tension of beam boundaries using
measured and simulated natural frequencies and damping ratios of a beam
3 Analyze eпђАects of boundary non-linearities on beam vibrations
4 Develop a script that applies identiпђБcation techniques [40] on time signals and test
identiпђБcation techniques on simpliпђБed beam models and vibration measurements.
This report begins with a theoretical section on the modeling of a beam with arbitrary
boundary conditions in Chapter 2. Chapter 2 also contains estimation of linear boundary
parameters, using simulated natural frequencies and damping ratios. At the end of Chapter
2, a general parameter estimation method is suggested, which is validated in Chapter 3.
The general method suggested in Chapter 2 is validated for both simulated and measured
vibration data in Chapter 3. Chapter 4 consists of applying system identiпђБcation techniques
on simulated vibration time signals from generic one degree of freedom models, in order to
identify beam boundary non-linearities.
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 2
Linear boundary parameter
estimation
In this chapter, the equations of motion for a simple beam are derived. Boundary conditions
for the beam are linear springs and dash-pots. Equations of motions can be converted into
an eigenvalue problem, where mode shapes and natural frequencies can be solved for. The
relationship between natural frequencies, mode shapes, damping ratios and corresponding
boundary stiпђАness and damping parameters are analyzed. Estimation techniques are de-
veloped, for estimating boundary stiпђАness and damping parameters and axial tension from
natural frequencies, mode shapes and damping ratios.
The chapter starts with the stating an eigenvalue problem that describes the vibrations
of a beam with linear springs and dash-pots as boundary conditions. Then the following
estimation methods are presented:
1 A boundary stiпђАness estimation method, for when only one boundary conditions is un-
known, in which case estimation of this boundary conditions stiпђАness parameter from
measured natural frequencies becomes a linear regression problem. The beam case an-
alyzed is also used to illustrate natural frequencies dependency on boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ
2 An axial tension estimation method, which is a proposed method for estimating beam
tension from measured natural frequencies when boundary conditions are unknown,
without having to also estimate boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ. The beam case analyzed is also
used to illustrate natural frequencies dependency on beam tension.
3 A simultaneous stiпђАness and tension estimation method, which uses measured natural
frequencies and mode shapes to state a problem of linear regression involving boundary
stiпђАnessвАЩ and tension parameters using RayleighвАЩs quotient for an undamped eigenvalue
problem. This method is mostly used to illustrate a simpliпђБed version of method 4.
4 A boundary damping estimation method, which analogous to method 3 uses measured nat-
ural frequencies, damping ratios and mode shapes to state a problem of linear regression
involving boundary damping parameters using RayleighвАЩs quotient for a damped eigen-
value problem. This is the only method for estimating boundary damping parameters
presented in this report.
5
==PAGE:18==
6
CHAPTER 2. LINEAR BOUNDARY PARAMETER ESTIMATION
5 Finally, a general method for simultaneous stiпђАness and tension estimation without any
approximations is presented. This method is a non-linear regression problem, and can
be used with ether natural frequency or mode shape data or both.
The conditions for methods 1-2 are restrictive and methods 3-4 requires thorough measure-
ments of mode shapes, which can be diпђГcult in practice. However, methods 1-3 are used to
illustrate boundary stiпђАness estimation issues and understand natural frequency dependen-
cies on boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ on simple cases before a general method (method 5) is presented.
From an experiment, if stiпђАness parameters can be estimated from method 5, then mode
shapes can be estimated, which allows method 4 to be used to estimate damping. Hence
method 4-5 present a complete method for estimating tension and boundary stiпђАness and
damping parameters.
2.1
Equations of motion
In this section, the equations of motions of a beam in tension and with arbitrary boundary
conditions will be stated. All information in this section can be found in other works as well
[1,61-62].

2.2.1.

generate the same natural frequencies.
Since (2.58) is a fourth order equation in stiпђАnessвАЩ, it could be that there are up to 4
diпђАerent sets of stiпђАnessвАЩ that generate the same natural frequencies. At least 2 instances
are known, where 4 diпђАerent sets of stiпђАness generate the same natural frequencies. One
instance is the free-hinged beam with no tension. It has the same natural frequencies as the
hinged-free, the clamped-hinged and the hinged-clamped beam [1]. Another instance is the
free-guided beam with no tension. It has the same natural frequencies as the guided-free, the
clamped-guided and the guided-clamped beam [1].
==PAGE:29==
2.2. EIGENVALUE PROBLEM
17
Also, the free-free beam has the same natural frequencies as the clamped-clamped beam,
even though that is not just swapping boundaries between x = 0 and ax = 1. Similarly, the
hinged-hinged beam has the same natural frequencies as the guided-guided beam. No general
relationship has been found, that can relate a set of stiпђАnessвАЩ to 3 other sets of stiпђАness that
generate the same natural frequencies. This relationship is not believed to exist in general,
so that there are only two sets of stiпђАnessвАЩ that generate the same natural frequencies for all
but a few special cases.
2.2.2.
Added mass and 3D-considerations
Up till now, only transverse bending of a homogeneous beam in one direction has been
considered, but real beams have non-homogeneous mass and bending stiпђАness distributions
and can transversely vibrate in two directions. Some boundary types, like bolted joints, may
add mass and rotational inertia to the beam boundaries. It seems then, that if one wishes
to characterize a boundary, it is necessary to identify stiпђАness, damping and inertia at the
boundary. The rest of this report, however is dedicated to identifying stiпђАness and damping
eпђАects. The reason is that added mass from e.g. a bolt-head is straightforwardly calculated
from material and geometrical data, and its eпђАect can then be included by containing extra
boundary inertia in a non-homogeneous mass distribution ѕБA(x) at x = 0 and x = 1.
Another point to note is that real vibrations are in three dimensions, and there are two
directions of transverse vibrations. If boundary stiпђАness and damping parameters would be
estimated from measured natural frequencies and/or mode shapes (as will be done later in
this chapter), the question is raised whether a natural frequency belongs to bending modes
in one direction, or the other. If the beam in question is rotationally symmetric, the same
natural frequencies exist in both directions, except when boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ or damping are
direction dependent. In fact, for any direction of transverse vibration, any angle between 0
and 360 degrees may have diпђАerent stiпђАness and damping characteristics. This increases the
amount of parameters necessary to characterize a beam boundary, even in the linear case,
but in principal estimation of stiпђАness and damping parameters could be accomplished with
a more complicated model that incorporates bending vibrations in all directions. This will
not be explored in this report, however.
Equilibrium equations of beams with bending in two directions can be derived similarly
to the case with bending in one direction, which gives [66]
M вА≤вА≤

The estimate (2.90) should be more noise-robust than (2.88) for low stiпђАness k2, but will
analogously to (2.88) be sensitive to noise for high stiпђАness k2 - because there is division by
xj in (2.90) and xj will be close to zero for high stiпђАness (can be seen from (2.89)).
2.3.2.
Summary
In this section, it was found that boundary stiпђАness estimation can be posed as a linear
regression problem, when only one stiпђАness parameter is unknown, and tension is known. It
was also found that natural frequencies have low sensitivity to changes in boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ
when stiпђАness values are either very high (being essentially inпђБnite) or very low (being essen-
tially zero). But there are ranges of boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ where natural frequencies are very
sensitive to changes in boundary stiпђАness, and this range is diпђАerent for each vibration mode.
Next will be presented a method estimating tension when boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ are un-
known.
2.4
Tension estimation, using higher order modes
In this section, a simple technique for axial force estimation is suggested. Gauss-Newton
estimation techniques are [57] used.
The theory of this section can be understood without the transcendent equation. Mode
shape ѕЖj of mode j is given by (2.47) as
ѕЖj(x) = C1 cos(ќ±1jx) + C2 cos(ќ±1jx) + C3 cosh(ќ±2jx) + C4 sinh(ќ±2jx),
(2.91)
where ќ±1j and ќ±2j are the ќ±1 and ќ±2 values of mode j. It is often found experimentally,
that mode shapes are very close to being a standing sinusoidal wave with an integer number
of half wavelengths. That means mode j has around j half wavelengths and mode j + 1
has around j + 1 half wavelengths. The number of half wavelengths j is controlled by ќ±1j,
because this parameter is in the sine and cosine terms. Adding half a wavelength from j to
j + 1 is equivalent to adding ѕА from ќ±1j to ќ±1(j+1), since ѕА is half of 2ѕА. The latter argument
can written as the approximate relation,
ќ±1(j+1) вИТќ±1j вЙИѕА,
(2.92)
which is not exact, because mode shapes arenвАЩt in general pure sinusoidal waves. If (2.92)
is accurate, it can be used to estimate non-dimensional axial force p through measurements
of frequencies. In order to test (2.92), the пђБrst four ќ±1вАЩs of a clamped-clamped beam, a
clamped-hinged beam and a cantilever beam are found [1] and presented in table 2.1. It can
be seen, that (2.92) is a good approximation for higher order modes, but even for low order

Calculations will be conducted to analyze the eпђАect of tension on natural frequencies and
to check the validity of (2.99). For a cantilever beam, k1 вЖТвИЮ, k2 = 0, k3 вЖТвИЮ, k4 = 0, the
пђБrst four natural frequencies ѕЙj have been calculated (through solving (2.58)) for a range
of tensions p. The natural frequencies are shown on Figure 2.5a. Furthermore, as a check
of (2.99), the 8 пђБrst eigenvalues ќ±1j have been calculated for two diпђАerent tensions - a low
tension p = 10вИТ4 and a high tension p = 104. Figure 2.5b shpws the obtained eigenvalues.
Figure 2.5a shows that tension has to be of a certain magnitude (p > 10вИТ1) for it to aпђАect
natural frequencies, and that higher order modes are only aпђАected by tension at large tensions.
Figure 2.5b shows that it doesnвАЩt matter if tension is large (p = 104) or low (p = 10вИТ4), the
eigenvalues ќ±1j are almost the same. Also, in agreement with (2.99), Figure 2.5b shows that
eigenvalues ќ±1j can reasonably approximated as a linear function of mode number j. The
slope of such a linear function is close to ѕА, as the straight line formed by ќ±1jвАЩs on Figure
2.5b is parallel to a reference line with slope ѕА. Since this linear relationship between ќ±1j and
j is seemingly independent on the size of p, it means that natural frequencies are aпђАected by
tension in such a way as to compensate for changes in ќ±1j due to increases in p. That means
an estimation procedure can be created, which пђБts a value p to a set of natural frequencies,
such that the eigenvalues ќ±1j obey (2.99). For such a procedure, information about boundary
stiпђАnessвАЩ is unnecessary for higher order modes, as their eпђАect have been condensed in ¬ѓќ± in
(2.99). It is believed that the slope of ќ±1j with respect to j is close to invariant to boundary
stiпђАnessвАЩ as made probable by the examples in table 2.1. Boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ change natural
frequencies ѕЙj, but not eigenvalues ќ±1j other than through ¬ѓќ±.
2.4.1.
Estimation procedure
In this subsection, (2.99) will be used to пђБt a value for p.
Remembering the deпђБnitions of ќ±1 and ќ±2 in (2.46), it can be seen that for high enough
frequencies ќ±2 вЙИќ±1 so without idealized boundaries and with non-zero axial force, still the
expression (2.98) goes to something very large for high frequencies.
If stiпђАnessвАЩ were known, (2.97) could be used to evaluate the approximation tan ќ±1 =
constant and hence (2.92). If stiпђАnessвАЩ are not known, it is not known for which set of modes
(2.92) is valid. On Figure 2.3b of previous section, it was observed that for a high boundary
stiпђАness (¬ѓk2 = 1000), some modes behaved as if the stiпђАness was inпђБnite (j < 3), while
higher order modes behaved as if the stiпђАness was zero (j > 4). For modes j where this jump
happens, (2.92) will not be valid.
There is reason to be critical of (2.92), but for now the formula will be assumed to hold.

beam conпђБguration. The relation (2.92) will also be close to be exact when all modes used
in (2.103) are either so stiпђАthat a boundary stiпђАness is a zero-stiпђАness in comparison or the
modes are so soft that a boundary stiпђАness is inпђБnite in comparison. So, if the пђБtted ЋЖ¬ѓќ± is
close to one of the values in Table 2.2, that would indicate that the assumptions used to
derive (2.92) through (2.97) are okay. If ЋЖ¬ѓќ± is not close to any of the values in Table 2.2, that
indicates that probably (2.92) does not hold. If the пђБtted ЋЖ¬ѓќ± is close to that of a free-free
beam, ¬ѓќ± = ѕА
2, that indicates that all the modes used in (2.103) have so high stiпђАness, that
boundaries can be considered вАЭfree-freeвАЭ.
Since (2.92) only holds for higher order modes, the weighting will be chosen wj = 0 for
modes j = 1, 2, 3, 4 and wj = 1 for j > 4. It is unknown for which modes (2.92) might hold.
The range j > 4 is chosen, because (2.92) is a good approximation for beams with ideal
boundary conditions for j > 4 (can be seen from table 2.1).
2.4.2.

For a hinged-hinged beam with tension p, (2.92) will not be an approximate relationship,
eigenvalues can be shown from to be ќ±1j = ѕАj for all modes, even in the presence of tension.

In conclusion repeated uses of (2.109) and (2.110) does not in general estimate tension well.
But ranges of tension dependent on boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ, procedure (2.109-2.110) accurately
estimates tension within an order of magnitude. The procedure (2.109-2.110) is believed to
fail at low values of tension, due to tensions not aпђАecting natural frequencies much at low
tensions, as seen from Figure 2.5.
To understand why the procedure (2.109-2.110) also fails for tensions higher than p = 103,
one can consider the free-free transcendent equation in (2.98). For low values of tension p, the
eigenvalues ќ±1 and ќ±2 are very close, and hence tan ќ±1 вЙИвИТвИЮ, yielding ќ±1j = вИТѕА
2 + jѕА. But
for high values of tension, it can be deduced from (2.46) that ќ±2 >> ќ±1, so that (2.98) gives
tan ќ±1 = 0, which solves as ќ±1j = jѕА. So in this case, changing the tension from something
low to something high also changes ¬ѓќ± from вИТѕА
2 to 0.
In general there are errors between estimations and true values of p on the cases in Figure

right hand sides of (2.99) are subtracted, a measure of error is recovered,

2.4.3.
Summary
In this section, it was found that tension estimation can in some cases accomplished without
any knowledge of boundary conditions. It cannot be known from measurements whether
or not the proposed method works, which limits its use. If axial is very high, though, the
proposed method always estimates within an order of magnitude at worst. In general, it
was also found that natural frequencies are insensitive to changes in tension at low tensions,
which also explains why the proposed method works best for high tensions.
In this section was presented a method for estimating tension independent of boundary
stiпђАnessвАЩ, but in the next section, a method for estimating tension and boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ
simultaneously, taking into account both of their eпђАects.
2.5
StiпђАness and tension estimation using mode shapes

assumption that natural frequencies and mode shapes are known. It turns out that esti-

estimating them from the transcendent equation (2.58). The estimation method in this sec-
tion consists of пђБtting unknown parameters to an expression for RayleighвАЩs quotient [1], using
linear regression [5,57,63]. The choice of пђБtting boundary stiпђАness parameters and tension to
an expression for RayleighвАЩs quotient is suggested here.
If a mode shape is only known for a limited range of x, a technique for пђБtting a complete
mode shapes (and estimating p in the progress) is described by Rebecchi et. al [30].

2.5.1.
Validation of estimation procedure
The estimation procedure of (2.127) is tested on simulated natural frequencies and mode
shapes, generated by various combinations of parameters. Natural frequencies are obtained
through solving (2.58) and mode shapes are found by minimizing (2.67) to get coeпђГcients
C, which are then put into (2.47).
The integrals in (2.118) and (2.119) were calculated
analytically, using C, ќ±1 and ќ±2 inserted into (2.47). For tests of (2.127), only 5 simulated
natural frequencies were used. More could have been used, but it was found that using more
simulated natural frequencies decreased the accuracy of estimation via (2.127).
Figure (2.7) illustrates estimations of stiпђАness and tension, for p = 10вИТ1, k2 = 100, k3 =
101, kвИТ1
4
and 10вИТ20 < k1 < 1020.
The x-axis of all graphs is the true stiпђАness k1.
Both
estimations and true values of stiпђАnessвАЩ and tension are illustrated. Estimations on Figure
(2.7) are generally in good agreement with true values, but k1 is not estimated correctly when
either it is very high or very low.
Other simulations have been carried out, but not shown, where other parameters than k1
were varied. The same results were found as in Figure 2.7, the estimation procedure (2.127)
works when parameters are neither too low nor too high. When parameters are too high or
too low, the estimation errors are up to several orders of magnitude error from true values,
and sometimes boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ are estimated to be negative.
A potential explanation for wrong estimations, when boundary stiпђАness parameters are
very high, is that mode shape values at end-points are then very small, essentially zero. That
means that when e.g. k1 is very large, the mode shape at its degree of freedom, ѕЖj(0) is close
to zero for all modes j. Then the second column of B in (2.119) is zero, meaning its linearly
dependent on the other columns. This latter condition makes k1 non-identiпђБable [57].
A potential explanation for wrong estimations, when any parameter is small, is that
numerical error becomes comparable to the parameters value.
An explanation for why estimations are wrong when tension p is large has been sought
after. It has been found, by debugging scripts, that mode shape estimation using high values
of p results in all mode shapes being essentially exponential, with no harmonics. However,
in theory large tension means that the eпђАects of bending stiпђАness are negligible, turning the
equation of motion (2.28) into вИТpuвА≤вА≤ = ¬®u. Then the mode shape solution (2.47) contains only
trigonometric terms (no hyperbolic functions) - this corresponds to a case with C3 = C4 = 0.
It is believed that the script in Appendix C.2 estimates C3 and C4 erroneously, due to their
true values being very small. Then insertion these wrong C3 and C4 into (2.47), it can be seen
they are multiplied by cosh ќ±2x and sinh ќ±2x - but for large p, ќ±2 is also large (as seen from
(2.46), and the hyperbolic functions become very large. Then the calculated mode shapes
are contaminated by a numerical error that is being multiplied by something large, leading
to wrong estimations for high values of p.

Other explanations to erroneous estimations when parameters are very high or very low
are possible. From Figure 2.3 of previous section, it was found that eigenvalues ќ±1 and hence
natural frequencies are not sensitive to changes in boundary stiпђАness when this stiпђАness is
either very high or very low. It is noted that this could also be a reason to why estimations
through (2.127) are erroneous when boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ are very high or very low.
2.5.2.
Summary
In this section, it was found that tension and boundary stiпђАness estimation can be posed as a
problem of linear regression, and thus can be estimated simultaneously using simple methods.
The proposed method in this section requires knowledge of both natural frequencies and
mode shapes, however, which limits its use, as mode shapes are more diпђГcult to measure
than natural frequencies alone. But should mode shapes be known accurately, the proposed
method in this section returns correct estimates of boundary stiпђАness and tension, given these
parameters are not either too high or too low. This makes sense, as in previous sections, it
was found that natural frequencies were insensitive to changes in stiпђАness parameters at high
or low stiпђАnessвАЩ.
In the following section, it will be shown how the methods of this section, using RayleighвАЩs
quotient to relate boundary parameters to measurements, can also be used for damped mode
shapes for relating boundary damping parameters to measured damping ratios.
2.6
Damping estimation using mode shapes
In this section, boundary damping will be related to damping ratios, then an estimation
procedure will be suggested for estimating boundary damping parameters from damping
ratios.
A derivation of an expression for damping ratios from RayleighвАЩs quotient [1] is
suggested. The estimation method in this section consists of пђБtting unknown parameters to
an expression for damping coeпђГcients, using linear regression [5,57,63].
In principle, the methodology of this section is about пђБtting boundary damping param-
eters to a theoretical relation between modal damping coeпђГcients and boundary damping
parameters. This is complicated expression involving non-linearities in boundary and stiпђА-
ness damping parameters, but can be condensed into a linear form using measured mode
shapes. The suggested method for estimating damping parameters will look as if damping
coeпђГcients of each mode are only dependent on mode shapes and boundary damping pa-
rameters, which is true, but within вАЩmode shapesвАЩ lie a complicated dependency on stiпђАness
parameters, tension and boundary damping parameters.

2.6.1.
Estimation procedure
For estimation of damping parameters based on measured damping ratios, it has been found
by experimentation with estimation methods, that a numerically stable estimation procedure
is to пђБt damping parameters to an expression for damping coeпђГcients. Damping coeпђГcients
can be found by inserting damping ratios (2.149) into an expression for damping coeпђГcients,

2.6.2.
Validation of estimation procedure
A validation of the presented estimation procedureвАЩs robustness to noise has been carried
out. For a case with boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ k1 = 103, k2 = 10вИТ3, k3 = 103, k4 = 10вИТ3, damping
parameters c1 = 1, c2 = 10вИТ1, c3 = 1, c4 = 10вИТ1 and varying tensions.
Higher damping
parameters has been chosen for c1, c3 because this represents the boundary with high stiпђАness,
and one would expect higher damping forces where there are higher stiпђАness forces. Damping
coeпђГcients have then been derived from (2.153), after which measured damping ratios dmj
have been added noise by pulling them from normal distributions

parameter estimates can be achieved. Then, by taking the square root of these variances,
standard deviations can be derived. Given parameter estimates, a 96% conпђБdence interval
can be constructed [63] for each estimate using ¬±2 standard deviations.
Figure 2.8 shows damping parameter estimates with 96% conпђБdence intervals using n = 8
measured damping coeпђГcients. For the speciпђБc case, it can be seen from (2.8) that damping
through c2 and c4 are somewhat moderately estimatble, while estimates of c1 and c3 canвАЩt be
estimated accurately, due to their high variance. This is because the boundary at c1, c3 has
very large stiпђАness and hence small sizes of mode shapes. Damping boundary parameters
c1, c3 are thus not signiпђБcant on measured damping coeпђГcients, but could be, if c1 and c3
were much larger.
Figure 2.8 also shows how the large measurement errors on damping coeпђГcients propagate
into boundary damping estimates. Best case conпђБdence intervals on Figure 2.8 are at 20%
error. Measurement of damping coeпђГcients require both measurement of damping ratios and
natural frequencies, so errors on both measurements aпђАect damping coeпђГcient measurement.
With large realistic measurement errors on damping ratios, estimation of boundary damping
parameters can not be expected to be accurate.
2.6.3.
Summary
In this section, it was found that boundary damping estimation can be posed as a problem
of linear regression. The proposed method in this section requires knowledge of both natural
frequencies, damping ratios and mode shapes, however, which limits its use, as mode shapes
are more diпђГcult to measure than natural frequencies alone. But should mode shapes be
known accurately, the proposed method in this section returns correct estimates of boundary
damping, and the eпђАects of measurement errors eпђАect on estimation uncertainties can be
evaluated (so that an estimates signiпђБcance can be tested if needed).
If stiпђАness parameters are known, they can be used to estimate mode shapes, which then
can be used in the method of this section to estimate damping parameters. So far, however,
no general stiпђАness estimation technique has been presented that is not only works for limited
cases or only works when mode shapes are known. A general stiпђАness estimation method that
works in every case that Euler-Bernoulli beam theory holds, with or without mode shapes
will now be presented in the following section.
2.7
StiпђАness and tension estimation, general procedure
In this section, a general procedure for estimation boundary stiпђАness parameters and ten-
sion will be proposed, for when limited or no information about mode shapes is available.
The procedure presented in this section is a Gauss-Newton iteration scheme [57], that esti-
mates unknown parameters from measured natural frequencies.The Gauss-Newton method
is slightly modiпђБed to also incorporate information about mode shapes in addition to natural
frequencies. The found estimates of stiпђАness and tension parameters will be вАЭmaximum a
posterioriвАЭ (MAP) estimates[57]. Necessary partial derivatives are derived in this section.

Mode shape terms in (2.166) are multiplied by a factor ќ≥M, which describes the relative
weighting of mode shape errors relative to natural frequency errors. Since there are 2 sets
of parameters that estimate the same natural frequencies (each set of parameters has a
corresponding swapped boundary case), an iteration procedure based on minimizing (2.166)
may end in a local minimum at the wrong set. Mode shapes are though to make a set of
parameters which estimate both natural frequencies and mode shapes to be unique. For this
reason, ќ≥M should be very large in the start of an iteration and then go to 1 after some
time, so that parameter estimates are pulled toward the correct local minimum. Here, ќ≥M is
suggested to be

a posteriori weighting [57]. In (2.176), k is the number of passed iterations.

2.7.3.
Dealing with negative and inпђБnite parameters
In this subsection, a method for avoiding negative parameters and parameter divergence to
inпђБnity will be presented.
Sometimes the solution for вИЖЋЖP in (2.197) will admit negative parameters.
But it is
known that having negative stiпђАnessвАЩ or negative tension might (only sometimes) make beam
vibrations unstable, and then natural frequencies arenвАЩt properly deпђБned, so that Y and V
canвАЩt be evaluated for negative parameters. This method suggested here could be expanded
to include the range of allowable negative parameters, but this will not be done here.
On the other hand, if stiпђАnessвАЩ become very large, the calculated mode shapes and natural
frequencies wonвАЩt be very sensitive to changes in these stiпђАnessвАЩ. This eпђАect which was seen
from Figure (2.3), where natural frequencies computed at large stiпђАness are the same as
natural frequencies computed at inпђБnite stiпђАness. The insensitivity of natural frequencies
and mode shapes to large stiпђАnessвАЩ cause Y to be singular, so that solving (2.197) becomes
impossible.
For large tension p, it has been found from using programs to and mode shapes, that
calculated mode shapes have diverging terms. It is believed this is due to ќ±2 becoming very
==PAGE:65==
2.7. STIFFNESS AND TENSION ESTIMATION, GENERAL PROCEDURE
53
large, while the true coeпђГcients C3 and C4 correspondingly goes to zero, but the estimated
C3 and C4 donвАЩt go to zero as fast as they should.
If possible, using (2.197) to iterate into parameter spaces with negative parameters or
very large parameters should be avoided. This can be done in variety of ways (for example a
cost function that diverges around illegal parameter ranges), but here it will be done by пђБrst
checking after each iteration if any parameter is below a minimum tL or above a maximum

2.7.4.
Convergence
In the literature it is suggested to stop iterating the Gauss-Newton procedure when a es-
timated measured quantities are within some relative error of actual measured quantities
[57]. For the procedure tests in the next section, another approach proved successful. In this
approach, convergence is set to occur when S(ЋЖP) is close to the expected value of S(P). The
problem this solves is that there might not be any smooth minimum to S(P) that can be
detected, and if there is, it can take many iterations to пђБnd it, while not necessarily improving
parameter estimation, since S(P) might not have its global minimum at true minimum P
(due to measurement errors).

2.7.5.
ModiпђБcations to Gauss-Newton procedure
When the iteration procedure presented in this section was used as is, it did not converge very
well unless the initial estimate P0 was within a small percentage of true parameters P. The
iteration procedure iterated rapidly between several extremes and often stopped iterating at
local minimums.
A method, LevenbergвАЩs method [57] will be used to dampen oscillating parameter estima-
tions. The method consists of a strategic choice for U which is changing with each iteration
to smoothen the function S(P), thereby increasing the likelihood of converging to a global
minimum. When S(P) has several local minima, LevenbergвАЩs damped iteration method [57]
increases the chance of ending in the local minimum of S(P) closest to the initial a posteriori
estimate.
In LevenbergвАЩs method, the a posteriori estimate of P, ЋЬP is set to be equal to the current
estimate Pk. The a posteriori term in (2.196) has no eпђАect on V but does have an eпђАect on
Y in (2.195).

initially trusted, and gradually, measurements are trusted more.
When parameter estimates are far from the global minimum of S(P), the Levenberg-
Marquadt method is best, as it reduces the eпђАect of U each iteration, so there is less damping.

When Y is close to singular, the variance on parameters becomes very large. This occurs
when mode shapes and natural frequencies are insensitive to parameters, which is the case
for very low or very high stiпђАness as observed from (2.3) or for very low tension as observed
in (2.5). It is expected then, that presented iteration procedure does not provide accurate
results when stiпђАnessвАЩ are very high or low, or when tension is very low. The procedure
dampens iteration steps using h reduces variance if P is close to a true minimum to S(P).
If S(P) is close to being a real minimum at true parameters P, then the iteration procedure
is expected to be accurate since this would cause h to be low and the variance of parameter
estimation to be low.
2.7.7.
Summary
In this section, a procedure for estimating tension and boundary stiпђАness simultaneously
using Gauss-Newton iteration was presented. The method is ready to be used on cases to
check its validity.
The method is much more computationally complicated than any of the other methods
presented in this chapter, but the advantage is its generality. That is what makes this method
the core focus of this report and the following chapter is dedicated to validating it. Hence,
the elements of this section will be discussed further in the following chapter.
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 3
Validation of general estimation
procedure
This chapter is dedicated to validating the general estimation procedure suggested in section
2.7.
3.1

In this section, the general estimation procedure of previous section will be validated on
a few cases. The пђБrst case will be an almost cantilever beam with a tension so high that

measurements will also be investigated on the described cantilever beam-case. The second
case is one which has пђБnite stiпђАnessвАЩ at boundaries and almost no tension. Finally, a discussion
of damping estimation is presented in context with stiпђАness estimations, but for tests of
damping estimation methods, the section on damping estimation is referred to.

It is known that the true parameters would not be obtained through even more iterations,
since the sum of square of errors at true parameters is higher than at estimated parameters
(seen from table 3.4). That is, there is not a global minimum for S(P) at true parameters,
when there is excessive uncertainty.
The estimated natural frequencies shown in table 3.3 are still reasonably close to measured
natural frequencies, given the measurement uncertainty, despite estimation error of parame-
ters. The estimated natural frequencies are in some cases closer to true natural frequencies
than are the measured natural frequencies (seen from table 3.3).
Closeness of measured and estimate natural frequencies reveals why parameters are esti-
mated erroneously, it is because natural frequencies arenвАЩt sensitive to some parameters at
this range. For example, it doesnвАЩt matter if k3 was estimated to 1000 or 10000, it can just be
considered вАЩinпђБniteвАЩ as in Figure 2.3. Similarly, it doesnвАЩt matter if k2 is estimated to 0.001
or 4.8e вИТ6, it is simply just as if it is вАЩzeroвАЩ, as in Figure 2.3.

3.1.3.
Mode shapes with small uncertainty
Another test is conducted using the iteration procedure of previous section with ѕГѕЖ = 10вИТ2
and W = 0, as to simulate a case with small uncertainty on mode shapes and lack of measured
natural frequencies. The size of the mode shape uncertainty ѕГѕЖ = 10вИТ2 is not small however,
but it corresponds roughly to the numerical standard deviation of mode shape estimation.
Estimating mode shapes is a non-linear and non-exact procedure, particularly the part where
(2.67) is minimized to obtain C, which is the reason for the numerical uncertainty. So when
simulating measurements of mode shapes, this was done with some inaccuracy described by
roughly ѕГѕЖ = 10вИТ2. It is not known how to calculate the mode shapes with less variance, so
for this section ѕГѕЖ = 10вИТ2 will be considered to be вАЩno uncertaintyвАЩ

3.1.7.
Parameter estimation variance issues
It was found from the simulations covered in this section that uncertainty induced signiпђБcant
errors on parameter estimation. From (2.244) it is estimated that estimation variance is
occurs when Y(ЋЖP) is close to being singular, which occurs when natural frequencies and
mode shapes are simultaneously insensitive to change in a parameter. From Figure 2.3 and
Figure 2.5, the sensitivity of natural frequencies are close to zero at very low or very high
stiпђАnessвАЩ and at very low tension. This conпђБrms the pattern of the simulations in this section,
where tension was repeatedly estimated accurately, while large stiпђАness were estimated large
but within an order of magnitude and small stiпђАnessвАЩ were estimated small. That means,
that if stiпђАnessвАЩ are estimated very large, they should just be considered inпђБnite, since their
exact value doesnвАЩt matter much. if stiпђАnessвАЩ are estimated very small, they should just be
considered zero, since their exact value doesnвАЩt matter much. Also, small tensions should not
be accurately estimable.

CHAPTER 3. VALIDATION OF GENERAL ESTIMATION PROCEDURE
For this section, since MarquadtвАЩs choice of U was found to slow iteration considerably in
the previous example, the iterations in this section are accomplished by setting ќїM = 0, but
keeping ќїL as in the previous case, so that U = 0 far away from the global minimum and
is signiпђБcant close to the global minimum. Also the convergence parameter ќі2 was changed
to 10вИТ4, because the iteration was found to converge far away in parameter space from the
global minimum.
Furthermore, Y(ЋЖP) can also become singular if ЋЖk1 = ЋЖk2 or ЋЖk3 = ЋЖk4. These considerations

3.1.8.
Boundary damping estimations
If accurate parameter estimation of stiпђАness parameters and tension can be accomplished
through the iteration procedure of previous section, true mode shapes ѕЖj(x) can be estimated
on analytical form, even if they are not measured. Then, they can be put into Bc in (2.161)
along with measured damping coeпђГcients to obtain damping parameter estimates.
The
described procedure for estimating boundary damping parameters will be illustrated with a
single example with and with small uncertainty.
A numerical experiment is set up with k1 = 103, k2 = 10вИТ3, k3 = 103, k4 = 103, p =
102, c1 = 1, c2 = 0.1, c3 = 1 and c4 = 0.1. From true boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ and tension, true
natural frequencies ѕЙj and true mode shapes ѕЖj(x) are generated and used to calculate true
Bc in (2.154). Then, combining with true boundary damping parameters, formula (2.154)
can be used to generate true damping coeпђГcients dj of the пђБrst n = 8 modes. By using the
relation, ќЊ = d/(2ѕЙ), true damping ratios ќЊj are recovered.

distribution as

matrix of Pc in (2.163). The 96% conпђБdence intervals in table 3.12 show that c1, c2 and c4
are estimated with high conпђБdence, while c3 is estimated with almost no conпђБdence. The
reason seems to be that the eпђАect of c3 is not signiпђБcant on the damping coeпђГcients due to
the left boundary almost being a clamp k3 = 103, allowing little rotational, and hence little
damping from rotation. Hence c3 cannot be estimated accurately in the given case. If c3 was
larger, probably it would have a signiпђБcant eпђАect on damping coeпђГcients.
The conпђБdence intervals in table 3.12 could be useful for accessing the accuracy and signif-
icance of estimates of boundary damping parameters in пђБeld experiments. But the conпђБdence
intervals only take into account variability due to uncertainty on measured damping ratios.
The constructed conпђБdence intervals do not take into account variability due to erroneous
estimations of natural frequencies or ЋЖBc. Since the conпђБdence intervals in table 3.12 do not
take into account all sources of variability, which is the reason why there is estimated a conпђБ-
dence interval of zero on the case with no uncertainty on damping ratio measurements using
estimated stiпђАness parameters. Clearly, estimated damping parameters based on estimated
stiпђАness parameters with no measurement uncertainty on damping ratios are not estimated
to true values c1 = 1, c2 = 0.1, c3 = 1, c4 = 0.1.
The estimated stiпђАness parameters ЋЖP in table 3.12 were taken from a case where both
natural frequencies and mode shapes were used to пђБt stiпђАness parameters, so that natural
frequencies and mode shapes must be well represented by these stiпђАness parameters. The
estimated stiпђАness parameters are shown to be suпђГcient in determining c1, c2 and c4, despite
estimation errors on stiпђАness parameters.
This is due to boundary damping estimation

parameters, and the stiпђАness parameters are пђБtted exactly to estimate natural frequencies

To check the goodness of пђБt for the estimated boundary damping parameter estimates
received from the last column of table 3.12, the predicted damping coeпђГcients and damp-
ing ratios are compared to true and measured (including uncertainty) values in table 3.13.
Subscript m refers to measured values and hat accent refers to predicted values. Predicted
damping coeпђГcients are calculated through (2.154), while predicted damping ratios are cal-
culated through ЋЖќЊj = ЋЖdj/(2ЋЖѕЙj), where ЋЖѕЙj is the estimate of natural frequencies obtained from
estimated stiпђАness parameters.
Table 3.13 is presented here to validate the damping estimation procedures ability to pre-
dict decent estimates of damping ratios and coeпђГcients even in the presence of measurement
uncertainty and uncertainty in mode shapes and natural frequencies.
It has been illustrated through numerical experiments how damping parameter estimates
can be obtained from (2.161) even when mode shapes are unknown, as long as stiпђАness
parameters k1, k2, k3, k4, p are known accurately enough to estimate accurate mode shapes
and natural frequencies.
3.1.9.
Summary
So far, the general estimation procedure presented in section 2.7 has been validated for a few
beam cases.
It has been shown that estimating stiпђАness parameters based on natural frequencies with-
out uncertainty always gives the correct estimate. When uncertainty is present, this is results
in estimation errors, especially on parameters that are very high or very low, in which case
the iteration procedure can estimate wrong with several orders of magnitude. In any case
natural frequencies predicted from estimated parameters will be close to measured natural
frequencies.
For estimation without natural frequencies and with mode shapes with minimum uncer-
tainty turned out to also be quite accurate in estimating parameters, but not 100% accurate.

This is taken to be due to estimation uncertainty in the prediction of mode shapes from
estimated parameters. This uncertainty can be demonstrated numerically as giving slightly
diпђАerent mode shape coeпђГcients C every time C is estimated from the same parameters. It
is not known from where exactly this error is coming from, but one can expect that given the
amount of computations necessary for computing parameter steps ќіP, that small numerical
errors pile up, resulting in uncertainty.
In any case, including mode shapes with or without uncertainty in the iteration procedure
produces parameter estimates which estimates mode shapes close to measured values.
The iteration procedure presented cannot know the diпђАerence between two boundaries
without including mode shape information.
Estimation of boundary damping parameters, using mode shapes generated from stiпђАness
parameter estimates does work accurately. If the size of uncertainty is known for damp-
ing coeпђГcient measurements, it has been shown that the uncertainty of damping parameter
estimates can be evaluated, which allows one to discard some damping parameters as in-
signiпђБcant. Thus, these insigniпђБcant parameters cannot be known from the method used in
this section.
In total, the eпђАects of uncertainties that occur in a simulated environment on the accuracy
of estimation has been investigated. In the following section, the eпђАects of uncertainties that
may occur in a physical environment, by considering an experiment with a free-free steel
beam.
3.2
Validation on experimental data, free-free steel beam
The purpose of this section is to test some of the proposed estimation procedures on a real
case, a free-free beam, where it is very well known that boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ and tensions
are zero. Hence, since true parameters are known, estimations based on the free-free beam
allows one to compare estimated and real boundary stiпђАness parameters, and see the sources
of variations due to experimental setups, which were not evident from previous simulated
tests.
The advantage of this example, is that boundary stiпђАness and tensions are known (to be
0), but the disadvantage is that this is an extreme and non-typical case.

Which sets of parameters are correct? Those estimated based on natural frequencies, or

so the uncertainty of k1 = k2 = 0.2041 is large. Very similar natural frequencies would be
uncovered, for instance, if k1 = k2 = 0.1 or k1 = k2 = 0.01.

For the пђБrst two rigid body modes where mode shapes, natural frequencies and damping
ratios have not been measured, the пђБrst and second diagonal element in Wc can be set to
zero, corresponding to inпђБnite uncertainty (variance). For the measured modes and damping
coeпђГcients, proportional and boundary damping coeпђГcients ќ±,ќ≤,c12 and c34 are calculated
using (2.161), which gives the пђБrst column of table 3.19. The mean value for each mode
of the damping coeпђГcients in table 3.18 is used in (2.127). Using calculated variances, a
96% conпђБdence interval can be constructed for the parameter estimates using (2.162) after
considering the variance of a a mean value is n times lower than the variance of any value,
where n is the number of data used for estimating the mean. A 96% conпђБdence interval
consists, for a normal distribution, as 2 standard deviations.
Finally, ќ±, ќ≤, c12 and c34 have been estimated, which gives the пђБrst column of (3.19). It is
seen that there is signiпђБcant error on each estimate, but the estimate of c34 is negative with
a large conпђБdence interval in comparison to its numerical value, so it is insigniпђБcant with
signiпђБcance 4%.

It (c34) is removed from the model, and parameters ќ±, ќ≤ and c12 is estimated one more
time, this is the second column of table 3.19. The conпђБdence intervals of each measurement
has been drastically reduced, except for ќ±, which is not signiпђБcant at signiпђБcance 4%, since
its conпђБdence interval covers zero.

signiпђБcance 4%, since their conпђБdence interval does not cover zero. However, the uncertain-
ties in parameter estimates are still relatively large. To access the goodness of пђБt, the sum
of square errors weighted with Wc is considered. It is S = 15.5 for the пђБnal estimates of ќ≤
and c12. If c12 is dropped as well, S becomes 63 and if ќ≤ is dropped, S becomes 309. Since
there are 10 used measurements and each measurement is normalized with its approximate
variance, the expected value of S is equal to the number of measurements, E [S] = 10. Com-
paring S вЙИ10, it is seen that both 309 and 63 is very large, while the value of S = 15.5
indicates a good пђБt, as it is close to 10.

Since only translator damping at rubber bands c12 and stiпђАness proportional damping
is signiпђБcant, it means that damping originates primarily from stretching of rubber bands
and internal stiпђАness proportional damping forces in the beam. Damping due to rotation of
rubber bands or due to such eпђАects as air resistance (which is assumed mass proportional)
are insigniпђБcant.
From the damping ratio data in table 3.18, it can be seen that the three experiments often
recovered very diпђАerent damping ratios for the same mode. Figure 3.14 shows measured
damping ratios graphically for the three experiments and the mean damping ratios with
conпђБdence intervals. However, the variation in measurements between the three experiments
is likely not due to random eпђАects, but due to qualitative diпђАerences between the three tests.
The accelerometer test measures damping from transient vibrations, but changes the beam
dynamics by including accelerometer mass at some location. Measuring vibrations with laser
Doppler vibrometry would not included this eпђАect. The laser-shaker test also changes the
dynamics of the beam, because the shaker is jointed to the beam between its force sensor and
the beam, introducing a small link which may have some compliance and additional damping
mechanismвАЩs.
It is seen on Figure 3.14 that the parameter estimates in table 3.19 accurately predict
damping ratios for measurements with high conпђБdence, but inaccurately predicts damping
ratios for measurements with low conпђБdence.
Taking ќ≤ and c12 to be signiпђБcant, they can be estimated for the three experiments,
which gives the parameters listed in table 3.20. It is seen that stiпђАness-proportional damping
is consistently estimated around 9вИТ10¬Ј10вИТ5, while in between the tests c12 is estimated very
==PAGE:103==
3.2. VALIDATION ON EXPERIMENTAL DATA, FREE-FREE STEEL BEAM
91
diпђАerently. There is also the explanation that the beam was displaced along the rubber bands
between each experiment, changing the position (and hence the mode shape at this point)
where damping is applied. It is noteworthy, however that c12 was estimated very similarly for
the accelerometer-hammer test and the laser-shaker test. However, looking at Figure 3.14,
the dataset that best suits the model prediction based on the parameters in table 3.20 is the
laser-hammer test. If one were to assume then, that the laser-hammer test is the one that
most closely follow the proposed model, (3.21), then it is seen from Figure 3.14 that even
in this case, the пђБtted parameters ќ≤ and c12 do not predict low order modes damping ratios
very well. This could be due to mass-proportional damping being omitted, or be because
the hammer impact has excited non-linearities through high amplitudes, leading to more
complicated damping mechanismвАЩs.

By including mass proportional damping ќ± in the damping model (3.21) for the laser-

пђБrst damping ratio. The estimated parameters are ќ± = 43, 5, ќ≤ = 5, 6 ¬Ј 10вИТ5 and c12 = 16, 5,
which is very diпђАerent from whats obtained in table 3.20, but within the same order of
magnitude. These parameters are taken to be the true parameters, and the parameters пђБtted
with other experiments (in table 3.20) must have other damping mechanismвАЩs than those
in the proposed model (3.21).
Some criticism is in order, however, as the пђБrst damping
ratio predicted by the parameters used in Figure 3.15a is above 1.
In that case, there
is an over-damped system, so no natural frequency should have been obtained.
Since a
natural frequency was obtained, it is concluded that the model (3.21) has some reason for
not being valid for the пђБrst mode. This could be due to non-linearity caused by high amplitude
vibrations or there being another linear damping mechanism present than proportional and
boundary damping. It should also be noted that the eпђАect of boundary damping is small for
most modes, visualized by Figure 3.15b, so it may be so small it is insigniпђБcant. Also seen
from (3.15) is that the prediction of high damping ratio of mode j вИТ2 = 1 is mostly due to
mass-proportional, so mass-proportional damping may only be an adequate damping model
for higher order modes than the пђБrst measured. Mass proportional damping may be due to
air resistance, which would imply that the lower order modes are aпђАected by air-resistance
diпђАerently than the higher order modes, which is not unrealistic.
In conclusion, there are probably diпђАerent damping present in the three experiments,
and the inconsistency of measured damping ratios leads one to not trust them, so estimated
damping parameters probably are not exact.

3.2.3.
Summary
In conclusion, the general estimation method for estimating boundary stiпђАness parameters
and tension works пђБne for the free-free steel beam experiment presented in this section.
The method for estimating boundary damping parameters did not work as well, but this is
thought to be due to damping mechanisms that are not modeled either through proportional
damping modeling or boundary damping modeling. Perhaps one could design an experiment
where boundary damping is dominant in comparison to other types of damping, so that
damping is primarily due to boundaries and hence can be modeled as in this section with
(2.151).
As for now, the boundary damping model, (2.151) remains unvalidated for the
experimental case.
So in conclusion of this chapter, the general estimation procedure has been validated for
stiпђАness estimation through simulated and free-free experimental data, while boundary damp-
ing estimation has been validated for simulated data. This concludes all linear parameter
estimations of this report.

Chapter 4
Non-linear boundary identiпђБcation
This chapter deals with the problem of identifying non-linearities (non parameter estimation).
As such, deducing form of an non-linearity (square, cubic, Coloumb friction ect.) from time
series data is the objective of this chapter. This will be done with a method for qualitative
identiпђБcation proposed by Kurt et. al [40] on simulated time series data. The time series data
used will be simulated from an equation of motion describing the dynamics of a homogeneous
beam with non-linear boundary conditions.
In order to justify a beam dynamics model,
equations of motion for a homogeneous beam with non-linear boundary conditions is derived
before any identiпђБcation procedures are tested.
4.1

damping from (2.129) and additional non-linear terms. The boundary loads are then

4.1.1.
One-mode approximation
In the next section, a method for qualitative identiпђБcation [40] of non-linearities will be
checked on a series of examples.
The пђБrst step of this method is to use empirical mode
decomposition to decompose a multi degree of freedom signal into a пђБnite set of intrinsic mode
functions, each (in theory) representing one of the degrees of freedom present in the original
signal. This decomposition is necessary, because the rest of the qualitative identiпђБcation
method [40] revolves around analyzing a one-degree of freedom signal.
In this chapter, the eпђАects of multiple degrees of freedom will not be explored, instead
several non-linearities of note - cubic stiпђАness, square damping, dry friction - will be simulated
on a one degree of freedom system, to see their eпђАects isolated from decomposition errors.
On real signals, this decomposition is necessary to proceed, but here only the пђБrst mode of
a beam will be considered.
In that case, (4.11) is truncated at n = 1, so that u(x, t) = q(t)ѕЖ(x) (subscript 1 has been
dropped for simplicity). The resulting equation of motion (B.12) becomes

4.2. METHOD FOR QUALITATIVE IDENTIFICATION
99
4.2
Method for qualitative identiпђБcation
This section tests a technique suggested by Kurt et. al [40] for qualitative system identiпђБ-
cation of non-linearities. This method consists of processing time series data and comparing
the processed results with what they would have been, if the time series data was the result
of a linear oscillator. In this section, time series data will be simulated for a variety of linear
and non-linear oscillators, and results of KurtвАЩs method for qualitative system identiпђБcation
will be presented and discussed.
Consider the free vibrations of a one degree of freedom oscillator with a non-linearities
represented by a forcing f,
¬®q + 2ќЊѕЙ ЋЩq + ѕЙ2q = f(q, ЋЩq, t, ...).
(4.29)
The method of concern in this section [40] is to estimate components of f from a measured
signal x and use information about the evolution of f to identify whether or not non-linearity
is present in f.
The method consists of the following computational steps: First deпђБne a new signal ќ¶(t)
[40],
ќ¶(t) = eвИТjѕЙt( ЋЩq + jѕЙq),
(4.30)
which is related to the slow variations of q. For example consider a linear oscillation
q = eвИТќ±t cos(ѕЙt),
(4.31)
its associated ќ¶(t)-signal becomes
ќ¶(t) = (jѕЙ вИТќ± cos ѕЙt)eвИТќ±t
(4.32)
If the amplitude of q is slowly varying, the damping ќ± must be close to zero, so that ќ¶ вЙИ
jѕЙeвИТќ±t and so is describing the slow variations in the amplitude of q. This is useful because
if the form of slow variations ќ¶(t) is not of the form eвИТќ±t the signal is not a linear oscillation

v0 = 0.001 is chosen over e.g. v0 = 0 because the latter would cause trouble for the sign

oscillators is validated.

An attempt for an explanation for the two diпђАerent regions in Figure 4.6c will be presented.
For explaining, one considers equation (4.38) as being a linear oscillator
¬®q + 2ќЊѕЙ ЋЩq + ѕЙ2q = вИТ¬µ2,
(4.39)
when ЋЩq > 0 and
¬®q + 2ќЊѕЙ ЋЩq + ѕЙ2q = ¬µ2,
(4.40)
when ЋЩq < 0.
==PAGE:119==
4.2. METHOD FOR QUALITATIVE IDENTIFICATION
107
So actually a downward oscillation of q(t) will be a linear oscillation with equilibrium
q01 = вИТ¬µ2/ѕЙ2, while an upward oscillation of q(t) will be a linear oscillation with equilibrium
q02 = ¬µ2/ѕЙ2.
So when oscillations are large in amplitude, the equilibriums q01 and q02
are small in comparison so that they are essentially zero. Overall then, when oscillation
amplitudes are high, ln |ќЫ(t)| will behave as if q(t) was a linear oscillator with equilibrium
at q0 вЙИ0, so that ln |ќЫ(t)| is a linear function of time for large amplitudes. As amplitudes
become smaller, the diпђАerence between the equilibriums q01, q02 and zero becomes large
in comparison oscillation amplitudes, so that non-linearity should aпђАect q(t) more. Hence,
ln |ќЫ(t)| diverges from its linear trend as amplitudes become smaller.
However, ln |ќЫ(t)|
stagnates when amplitudes become small enough. This latter phenomenon invites to further
investigation.
The dry friction oscillator is simulated again for a large time span, and Figure 4.7a zooms
in on its region at high tвАЩs. It is seen from Figure 4.7a that q(t) stagnates at around q вЙИ10вИТ3,
at which point it seemingly slowly converges to some value which is not apparent from the
time series in Figure 4.7a. Several candidate hypothesis can be constructed to explain this.
One is that it is a product of numerical error, that the ordinary diпђАerential equation solver
used failed to converge properly. However, another explanation may be found, based on the
fact that the critical value for q where the stagnation takes place is close to its equilibrium
q02 = ¬µ/ѕЙ2 = 1.26 ¬Ј 10вИТ3.
It is hypothesized that the following process takes place: First, q(t) is above q02 = 1.26 ¬Ј
10вИТ3 with a negative ЋЩq, so that q02 is the equilibrium according to (4.40). Then q(t) passes
q02 so that q(t) is now below q02. Since ЋЩq is still negative, (4.40) will still be in eпђАect, so
q02 is an equilibrium and (4.40)вАЩs linear restoring force will pull q(t) in a positive direction
towards q02. If this restoring force is large enough to make ЋЩq(t) positive before q(t) passes
q01, q(t) will now be governed by (4.39), so that q(t) will be pulled towards q01 hence driving
its velocity to become negative again and making q(t) be pulled towards q02 again. So a limit
cycle has been reached.
However Figure 4.7a does not show a limit cycle (unless this cycle is very low frequency),
rather just a exponential convergence of some sort. So the explanation for the time signal
after t > 41 is still an open question. In any case, real dry friction has a stick phenomenon
at low amplitude oscillations, which is not modeled here. So the phenomenon past t > 41
might not arise in practice.

in a single simulation. This will approximate reality as best as will be done in this report,
if real beam dynamics can be modeled with a linear 1 DOF oscillator, with dry friction, a
square damping law and cubic stiпђАness.
Figure 4.8 shows the simulated time signal q(t), its frequency spectrum and its associated
forcing ln |ќЫ(t)|. Figure 4.8c shows that most of the phenomenon studied in this section can
