4.3
Summary
In this chapter, it has been shown how boundary forces and moments governed by cubic
stiпђАness, square damping and dry friction laws aпђАect the equations of motion for a beams
modal degrees of freedom. An identiпђБcation procedure [40] has been employed on simulated
time series data for a one-mode approximation of beam dynamics. Aforementioned procedure
consists of estimating an external force with complex amplitude ќЫ(t) and computing its
absolute value on a logarithmic scale, ln |ќЫ(t)|. For a 1 DOF pure linear oscillator, ln |ќЫ(t)|
is a linear function of time, but any non-linearity will cause ln |ќЫ(t)| to be a non-linear
function of time. The shape of ln |ќЫ(t)| for diпђАerent non-linearities have been investigated
and explained.
It was found that ln |ќЫ(t)| has a similar shape for a 1 DOF oscillator with a square damping
term as with a cubic stiпђАness term - that of added oscillations with decreasing amplitude.
The reason is that as time goes on, free vibrations tend to zero amplitude and cubic stiпђАness
and square damping forces become negligible next to linear stiпђАness and damping terms.
Negligibility at low vibration amplitude is a characteristic of many non-linearities (cubic
stiпђАness, quintic stiпђАness, quadratic stiпђАness, cubic damping...), and thus these non-linearities
cannot be told apart with the used identiпђБcation method so easily. Telling these apart seems
to require evaluations of of the convolution of ln |ќЫ(t)| and phase diпђАerences between ln |ќЫ(t)|
and q(t).
The eпђАects of multiple degrees of freedom in time signals have not been investigated, but
is covered in Kurt et. al [40] using empirical mode decomposition to decompose an N-degree
of freedom signal into N one degree of freedom signals.
==PAGE:123==
Chapter 5
Conclusions
In this report, methods for vibration-based identiпђБcation of beam boundaries have been
explored and validated. Some methods were already existent, but other have been suggested
here.
The case considered have been a linear homogeneous beam in tension with linear or non-
linear boundary conditions. The report is in two parts, Linear Boundary Parameter
Estimation: Using measured natural frequencies, mode shapes and damping ratios, to
estimate tension and boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ and damping parameters; Non-linear Boundary
IdentiпђБcation: Using displacement time series to qualitatively identify non-linear boundary
conditions described by square damping laws, cubic stiпђАnessвАЩ and dry friction. The main
focus of this report is on the former, Linear Boundary Parameter Estimation, where boundary
conditions have been considered linearized translational and torsional springs and dash-pots
Several methods have been suggested and validated for Linear Boundary Parameter Es-
timation:
1 One-parameter boundary stiпђАness estimation
2 Tension estimation
3 Simultaneous boundary stiпђАness and tension parameter estimation using mode shapes
4 Estimation of boundary damping parameter estimation
5 Simultaneous boundary stiпђАness and tension parameter estimation, general procedure
All the above methods are based on a least-square пђБtting procedure and have been validated
using simulated measurements, and Method 5 has been validated using measured data from
a free-free beam experiment. Each method requires some form of measurements, and uses
either linear or non-linear regression. What each method uses as input and what it generates
as outputs are summarized by Figure 5.1.
A single method,
6 Qualitative identiпђБcation using non-linear forcing amplitudes,
for qualitative non-linear Boundary IdentiпђБcation has been validated for non-linear boundary
conditions consisting of cubic stiпђАnessвАЩ, square damping and dry friction loads. This method
111
==PAGE:124==
112
CHAPTER 5. CONCLUSIONS
approximates non-linear forces as time harmonics with a slowly varying amplitude and phase,
and then uses measured time series data to estimate said amplitude. The force amplitude
time series then holds information about non-linearities.
It has been found that Method 1, one parameter stiпђАness estimation, works пђБne, but can
be sensitive to measurement uncertainties when stiпђАnessвАЩ are either very large or very small.
This has to due with the fact, that пђБtting parameters to data via a model that is insensitive
to changes in parameters wont be precise. That is, natural frequencies are essentially the
same the same for boundary stiпђАness of 0.1 and 0.00001 (essentially zero), or for boundary
stiпђАness of 1000 and 1000000 (essentially вАЭinпђБniteвАЭ). This method is restrictive, though, as
it requires one to know all but one boundary condition. Its advantageously based on linear
though, instead of non-linear regression.
Method 2, tension estimation using higher natural frequencies, works only when beam
stiпђАness due to axial tension is large in comparison to beam stiпђАness due to bending stiпђАness,
which is due to model predicted natural frequencies being insensitive to tension at low tensions
in comparison to bending stiпђАnessвАЩ, thus making any пђБtting procedure uncertain. For some
ranges of boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ this method works, but for some it doesnвАЩt. Since it cannot be
known beforehand whether or not if boundary stiпђАnessвАЩ is in the range that allows for this
method to work, this method for tension estimation cannot be used with certainty. Also, a
non-linear regression method is required.
Method 3 and 4, use an expression for RayleighвАЩs quotient for an undamped and a damped
eigenvalue problem respectively to derive a formula, from which tension and boundary stiпђА-
ness and damping parameters can be estimated using mode shapes, natural frequencies and
. The advantage of Method 3 and 4 over those of Method 1 and 2 (besides damping being
included in method 4, while not being included in Method 1 and 2), is that all boundary
stiпђАness and tension can be estimated simultaneously from linear regression procedure. The
disadvantage of Method 3 and 4 is the need to measure mode shapes. Again, in order to use
Method 3, boundary parameters must not be too large or too small as to make model pre-
dicted natural frequencies be insensitive to these parameters. Similar for Method 4, damping
boundary parameters must be signiпђБcant in their eпђАects on measured damping ratios in order
to be estimated accurately. Also, Method 3 and 4 cannot be used if two boundaries are iden-
tical, as the optimal parameter estimate is then non-unique according the set up least-square
criteria of these methods.
Method 4 has still been used to estimate boundary damping parameters even without
measured mode shapes, even though measured mode shapes are required. This is done by
estimating tension and boundary stiпђАness parameters using another method than Method
3 (such as Method 5), and then using those parameters to estimate mode shapes for an
undamped beam. These estimated mode shapes can then be used as inputs for Method 4
instead of measured mode shapes. So if tension and stiпђАness parameters can be estimated
without mode shapes, Method 4 can still be used to estimate boundary damping parameters.
Method 5 was found to work well for the cases analyzed in this report. Method 5 involves
an iterative procedure, and it was found that several numerical iteration tricks were necessary
in order to avoid divergence. Also, the iteration of Method 5 can some times take long.
Method 5 uses measured natural frequencies and mode shapes to пђБt boundary parameters
via a model. Like the other estimation methods mentioned here, Method 5 does not work
well, when parameters are either very high or very low, and does not work at all when
==PAGE:125==
113
Figure 5.1: Table of methods. *Either natural frequencies or mode shapes or both can be
used
==PAGE:126==
114
CHAPTER 5. CONCLUSIONS
measurements are uncertain. When this is not the case, Method 5 is robust to measurement
uncertainty, which has been checked for a few cases. Unlike Method 3 and 4, Method 5 can
still work when two boundaries are identical or very close to being so. This only works if
measured mode shapes are used for пђБtting as well as natural frequencies - mode shapes make
parameter estimates unique. If mode shapes are not used for пђБtting, Method 5 may estimate
boundary parameters which have the left and right beam boundary swapped, since natural
frequencies are the same for a mirrored beam (but mode shapes are not).
It has been found by using Method 6 on non-linear time series simulated on a beam
with non-linear boundaries, that it can be identiпђБed whether or not the measured vibration
signals underlying dynamics are linear or non-linear. Qualitative features on KurtвАЩs forcing
amplitudes stemming from measurement noise, пђБltering and several non-linearities have been
investigated. It can be concluded that the form of a non-linearity cannot be deduced easily
by looking at KurtвАЩs forcing amplitude graphs, since some non-linearities that are physically
very diпђАerent (e.g. square damping and cubic stiпђАness) have a similar forcing amplitude
evolution.
The essential contributions of this work are:
a The qualitative relations between natural frequencies and boundary stiпђАness illustrated on
Figure 2.3.
b The qualitative relations between natural frequency and tension illustrated on Figure 2.5
(although something similar can be found elsewhere).
c The analytical derivation for damping ratios dependency on boundary damping parame-
ters, equation 2.149
d Method 5 in conjunction with Method 4, which can be used on any beams to estimate
linearized boundary parameters and tension simultaneously. This method could maybe
be adapted to other cases than an Euler-Bernoulli beam, such as a Timoshenko beam
The equation of motion of a beams modal coordinates with non-linear boundary conditions
(B.12) could have been added to the list, but it is very possible it can be found elsewhere in
a similar form.
Based on the mentioned contributions a-d, the following recommendations for further
work on vibration based identiпђБcation of beam boundary models:
I Expression 2.149 and Method 4 and 5 should be validated experimentally on beams that
are not free-free, but have known boundary parameters
II Necessary пђБne tuning of iteration procedure would need to be carried out (avoiding diver-
gence in smarter ways, ensuring convergence more quickly, higher quality convergence
criteria) to accommodate problems with Method 4 and 5 that may occur with data
from an experiment
III Time series data from non-linear oscillators should be analyzed with Method 6, to see if
estimated forcing amplitudes show a pattern such as those in Figures 4.1-4.8c. Already
done on some cases with partial success, [40]
IV More powerful non-linear identiпђБcation techniques should be found

C.3
General stiпђАness and tension estimation
The code NatEstTest.m accomplishes 3 things:
1 Simulates natural frequencies and mode shapes with added uncertainties
2 Iterates into estimates of boundary parameters
3 Post-processing, generates iteration plots and illustrations of mode shapes
When experimental data is used instead of simulated data.
The пђБrst part of the script
should be modiпђБed to include experimental data and uncertainty assessments In addition to
the already mentioned codes, the following functions are used:
GenShape.m - generates mode shape coordinates (x, ѕЖ(x)) with random magnitudes
ScaleShape.m - scales an estimated mode shape to a measured one

gradomegas.m - calculates the sensitivity of natural frequencies to parameter changes as
a vector
gradomega.m - calculates the sensitivity of one natural frequency to parameter changes.
Used in gradomegas.m. Uses muCalc.m
SS phi.m - evaluates the part of S(ЋЖP) stemming from mode shape errors. Uses GenShape.m
and ScaleShape.m
VYbuilder.m - builds Yj and Vj stemming from a single mode shape
VYsum.m - sums contributions Yj and Vj from all mode shapes

wHann( j )=1/2вИЧ(1вИТcos ((2вИЧpi вИЧ( j вИТ1))/(NвИТ1))); % Hann window
wRect ( j )=1; % Rectangular window (= No window )
end

C.4. NON-LINEARITY IDENTIFICATION ROUTINE

% Define
the ODE function
%вИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТ
function
yPrime = ODE ParaPendulumEq( t , y ,m1,m2, gam , omega0 , beta
)
%вИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТ
% Calculate
the
r . h . s
of
the ODE written
in
standard
form :
y вАЩ = f (y , t , . . . ) .
% Here y1=u and y2=u вАЩ
yPrime (1 ,1) = y ( 2 ) ; %=uвАЩ=du/dt=v
yPrime (2 ,1) = вИТ2вИЧbeta вИЧomega0вИЧy (2) вИТ( omega0 ЋЖ2)вИЧy(1)вИТgamвИЧy (1)ЋЖ3
. . .
вИТsign (y ( 2 ) ) вИЧ(m1вИЧy(2)ЋЖ2+m2);%=u вАЩ вАЩ
end
function
[xFFTdB,
fFFT , nFFT,
fNyq ,
xPhase ] = fft dB (x ,N,T, dBmax ) ;
% Compute the FFT of
a
uniformly
sampled
time
s e r i e s ,
in
a form
t y p i c a l l y
% used
f o r
v i b r a t i o n
a n a l y s i s .
%
% INPUT
%
x :
Vector
containing
the
uniformly
sampled
time
s e r i e s
to
be FFTвАЩ ed
%
N:
Length
of
x
%
T:
Total
sampling
time
%
dBmax: Max dBвИТvalue
wanted
f o r
the FFT
%
% OUTPUT
%
xFFTdB:
Vector
containing
the FFT of
x ,
given
as
magnitude
( i . e .
|FFT( x ) | ) ,
%
and
scaled
to
a maximum of dBmax d e c i b e l .
%
xPhase :
Vector
containing
the
phases
( in
radians )
of
the FFT of
x
%
fFFT :
Vector
of
f r e q u e n c i e s
corresponding
to
the
elements
in xFFTdb
%
nFFT:
Number of
elements
in xFFTdb and fFFT
%
fNyq :
Nyquist
frequency (= l a r g e s t
value
in fFFT)
%
% NOTES:
% (1) No windowing
( e . g .
Hann ,
Hamming ,
exponential ,
etc . )
of
the
time
s e r i e s
i s
% applied ;
f e e l
f r e e
to window the
ti me s i gn a l
before FFTвАЩ ing
i t
%
% Jon
Juel Thomsen /
April
4 ,
2006
% вИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТвИТ
dt = T /
(NвИТ1); % Sampling
i n t e r v a l
f s = 1/ dt ; % Sampling
frequency
fNyq = 1/2вИЧf s ; % Nyquist
frequency
df = 1/T; % Frequency
r e s o l u t i o n
nFFT = N/2 + 1; % ( u s e f u l )
Length
of
the FFT ( the
remaining N/2вИТ1 points
are
not
used )
xFFT = f f t ( x ) ; % Use MATLABвАЩ s
function
f f t
to
Fourier
transform
the
time
s e r i e s
dBnorm = dBmax вИТ20вИЧlog10 (max( abs (xFFT ) ) ) ; % Constant
needed
f o r
c a l c u l a t i n g dB
% D e f i n i t i o n :
[dB] = 20вИЧlog ( SignalValue / SignalReferenceValue )
xFFTdB = 20вИЧlog10 ( abs (xFFT)) + dBnorm ; % For
d i s p l a y i n g
the FFT:
c a l c u l a t e
|xFFT|
in dB.
fFFT = 0
:
df
:
(nFFTвИТ1)вИЧdf ; % Frequency
l i n e s
corresponding
to
the FFTвИТcomponents
y = f f t ( x ) ;
% Compute DFT of
x
xPhase = unwrap ( angle (xFFT ) ) ;
end
